<div id="review_1835598382" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1835598382" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1835598382?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140291094" href="/book/show/883217.The_End_of_the_Affair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=fiction-general">fiction-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1835598382" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17676549947925238649">Love is in the air--or maybe anxiously repressed--in February and my romantic literature jag continues with <i>The End of the Affair</i>, the 1951 doomed romance by Graham Greene. This was my first exposure to Greene's fiction and while I was struck by the celebrated British author's intricate prose, keeping time like a Swiss clock, this novel is deficient in story, forgoing action for the reflections of its melancholy male narrator. These musings proceed from revealing to unceasing and finally, I just</span>
  <span id="freeText17676549947925238649" style="display:none">Love is in the air--or maybe anxiously repressed--in February and my romantic literature jag continues with <i>The End of the Affair</i>, the 1951 doomed romance by Graham Greene. This was my first exposure to Greene's fiction and while I was struck by the celebrated British author's intricate prose, keeping time like a Swiss clock, this novel is deficient in story, forgoing action for the reflections of its melancholy male narrator. These musings proceed from revealing to unceasing and finally, I just wanted <i>The End of the Affair</i> to end. The experience of reading this book was like listening to a 192-page voice mail message from an ex-boyfriend who hasn't moved on.<br><br>The story begins on <i>"a wet black January night on the Common, in 1946"</i> with our narrator, successful novelist Maurice Bendrix, signaling a fellow Londoner he recognizes in the rain. The man is Henry Miles, a civil servant whose contribution, as Bendrix sees it, is moving from assistant secretary in the Ministry of Pensions to assistant secretary in the Ministry of Home Security. Bendrix invites Henry for a drink out of morbid curiosity for news of Henry's wife Sarah, his ex-lover who Bendrix hasn't seen since June 1944 and never gotten over. Bendrix is full of barely repressed antipathy, both for Sarah and her pathetic husband, but as an author, is valued by Henry as a listener.<br><br>Unable or unwilling to consider that Bendrix's friendship with his wife might have developed into a love affair, Henry invites Bendrix to his home while Sarah is out for a walk. He confides that he's been deeply troubled by the fear that his wife might leave him. He shows Bendrix a letter in which a friend recommends a private detective. Bendrix offers to handle the transaction, musing <i>"Jealous lovers are more respectable, less ridiculous than jealous husbands. They are supported by the weight of literature. Betrayed lovers are tragic, never comic."</i> Henry burns the letter and tells Bendrix to forget it, but the writer has memorized the Vigo Street address of the private detective.<br><br><i>What an odd collection the trusted professions are. One trusts one's lawyer, one's doctor, one's priest, I suppose, if one is a Catholic, and now I added to the list one's private detective. Henry's idea of being scrutinized by the other clients was quite wrong. The office had two waiting-rooms, and I was admitted into one. It was curiously unlike what you would expect in Vigo Street--it had something of the musty air in the outer office of a solicitor's, combined with a voguish choice of reading matter in the waiting-room which was more like a dentist's--there were</i> Harper's Bazaar<i> and </i>Life<i> and a number of French fashion periodicals, and the man who showed me in was a little too attentive and well-dressed. He pulled me a chair to the fire and closed the door with great care. I felt like a patient and I suppose I was a patient, sick enough to try the famous shock treatment for jealousy.</i><br><br>After his interview with the private detective, Bendrix returns home and is notified by his landlady that he received a call from Mrs. Miles. Meeting for lunch, Bendrix compels Sarah to join him at Rules, their old restaurant. Sarah asks Bendrix to look her husband up occasionally, concerned he might be lonely. Bendrix manages to keep his bitterness under wraps, advising her to see a doctor for a cough he observes. He notices a man with a young boy in tow watching him and upon returning home, Bendrix is called on by this man, whose name is Parkis and has been assigned by the detective agency to report on Sarah's coming and goings.<br><br>Bendrix recalls being introduced to his beautiful and charming ex-lover by her husband, who he'd met at a party in the summer of 1944 and is curious to study for a future book. He invites Henry's wife to dinner for the purpose of picking her brain about her hapless civil servant husband. Walking Sarah down Maiden Lane to the tube station, Bendrix delivered a fumbling kiss without any expectation of going to bed with her. When the author invites Sarah to a screening of a movie based on one of his books, she comes without Henry. At dinner afterward, Maurice and Sarah fall in love over a bowl of onions.<br><br>Exempted from military service due to a bad leg, the war in Europe becomes a backdrop for Bendrix's obsession for Sarah, which proceeds through the air raids on London in June 1944. The lovers share little concern of their passion being exposed to Henry, but is tempered by their realization that ultimately, the affair will come to an end. Mr. Parkis determines that Sarah has been stealing away to an apartment at 16 Cedar Road. Bendrix learns that the tenant is a street preacher named Richard Smythe. Studying a diary which Parkis gets his hands on, Bendrix discovers Sarah is hardly carrying on an affair with Smythe, but seeking spiritual salvation.<br><br><i>He is jealous of the past and the present and the future. His love is like a medieval chastity belt; only when he is there, with me, in me, does he feel safe. If only I could make him feel secure, then we could love peacefully, happily, not savagely, inordinately, and the desert would recede out of sight. For a lifetime perhaps.<br><br>If one could believe in God, would he fill the desert? <br><br>I have always wanted to be liked or admired. I feel a terrible insecurity if a man turns on me, if I lose a friend. I don't even want to lose a husband. I want everything, all the time, everywhere. I'm afraid of the desert. God loves you, they say in the churches, God is everything. People who believe that don't need admiration, they don't need to sleep with a man, they feel safe. But I can't invent a belief.</i><br><br>Graham Greene writes with precision in the way he considers jealousy, with the corporeal and spiritual costs of a romance in which nothing is ever enough for one man. Even when playing out against atmospheric conditions like the rain and fog on a London winter, or an air raid with V1 rockets raining down on the city during one rendezvous, the novel's busiest activity takes place in the souls of the lovers. This approach might be most satisfying to those who enjoy introspective novels. One paragraph at a time, the writing is often jeweled, making up for an inherent lack of drama with language that dances across the page.<br><br><i>I am a jealous man--it seems stupid to write these words in what is, I suppose, a long record of jealousy, jealousy of Henry, jealousy of Sarah and jealousy of that other whom Mr Parkis was so maladroitly pursuing. Now that all this belongs to the past, I feel my jealousy of Henry only when memories become particularly vivid (because I swear that if we had been married, with her loyalty and my desire, we could have been happy for a lifetime), but there still remains jealousy of my rival--a melodramatic word painfully inadequate to express the unbearable complacency, confidence and success he always enjoys.</i><br><br>Where <i>The End of the Affair</i> grows putrid for me is when soul searching like this ceases to serve as commentary on what the characters are doing and instead becomes all the characters are doing. This sums up most of the novel, unfortunately. Chapters started dragging for me as soon as Greene typed the word "God" and started going on explorations of human frailty in the Catholic sense, which seems more suited to a sermon than a novel. His characters are passionate and tormented, so the novel isn't lifeless necessarily, just tedious. The religious discussions made me feel like I was holding a chisel against my head and the author was hammering it.<br><br>The film industry has returned to Graham Greene for material for nearly as long as there's been a film industry and <i>The End of the Affair</i> has been adapted to film twice. In 1955, a U.K. production directed by Edward Dmytryk starred Deborah Kerr as Sarah Miles, Van Johnson as Bendrix and Peter Cushing as Henry Miles. In 1999, Columbia Pictures mounted a version adapted and directed by Neil Jordan featuring Ralph Fiennes as Bendrix, Julianne Moore as Sarah Miles and Stephen Rea as Henry Miles. Perhaps intending to appeal to the same audience <i>The English Patient</i>, I far prefer that film's embrace of an affair during wartime rather than religious screed.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487628672i/22033813.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487629197i/22033867._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="17676549947925238649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2621689646" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2621689646" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2621689646?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=brittish-lit">brittish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2621689646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9983701812580679802">This is my first read of a book by Graham Greene. Two of his books are in my personal collection for about a year now, somehow or other being pushed behind other reads until now. I also need a little preparation of mind when I venture into a new author because I'm always nervous in such situations (this might sound absurd but it is true for me). However, after reading I felt like kicking myself for keeping the book so long without reading.<br/>	<br/>The first thing that drew me into the book is not the st</span>
  <span id="freeText9983701812580679802" style="display:none">This is my first read of a book by Graham Greene. Two of his books are in my personal collection for about a year now, somehow or other being pushed behind other reads until now. I also need a little preparation of mind when I venture into a new author because I'm always nervous in such situations (this might sound absurd but it is true for me). However, after reading I felt like kicking myself for keeping the book so long without reading.<br />	<br />The first thing that drew me into the book is not the story nor the characters, but Greene's intense and powerful writing. Greene displays such rawness, such openness, and such sincerity in his writing. This writing draws and ensnares the readers and imprisons them in his words. If you consider the story, it is pretty simple. But it is just a base for Greene to develop all the raw human emotions one feels when a relationship ends. I read that Greene's personal relationship with one Lady Walston inspired him to write this, so perhaps the personal experience speaks for the intensity of his writing. <br /><br />The story is simple and beautiful. The first-person narrator, who is also the male protagonist, tells his story of the end of an affair; and at the same time, he goes beyond his story, laying bare all his inner conflicting emotions to his audience. The story touches thematically on love, hate, pain, jealousy, obsession, life, death, and faith. Greene has worked strongly on the themes through the relationship of the male protagonist, the writer Maurice Bendrix and the female protagonist Sarah Miles, wife of a civil servant. <br /><br />There are very few characters in the novel. I believe I didn't like any of them. But I get this queer feeling that Greene was bent upon his characters' being understood rather than liked. If that was his goal, he did achieve it brilliantly. You may find it hard to like them, but you will certainly understand them; you will feel their emotions and you will pity them. <br /><br />The story was a sad one, yet I enjoyed it. The ending deviated from the thematic flow of the story creating an out of sorts effect, but the overall reading experience was satisfying. I could honestly say that Graham Greene has done very well with a common theme.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9983701812580679802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3428281571" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3428281571" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3428281571?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=british-novels">british-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=literary-novel">literary-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3428281571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1846618084720438590">There are so many angles with which this novel can be approached. Mine might be a little off-kilter, but here goes. The review contains spoilers.<br/><br/>I experienced the plot as the development of an unlikely friendship between two men who both were deeply in love with a women who had the ability to cast a spell over all the men she met. Bendrix was the lover, and our introspective protagonist. Henry was her husband, or pimp, as Bendrix would accuse him of in one of his jealous, yet heartbroken moments</span>
  <span id="freeText1846618084720438590" style="display:none">There are so many angles with which this novel can be approached. Mine might be a little off-kilter, but here goes. The review contains spoilers.<br /><br />I experienced the plot as the development of an unlikely friendship between two men who both were deeply in love with a women who had the ability to cast a spell over all the men she met. Bendrix was the lover, and our introspective protagonist. Henry was her husband, or pimp, as Bendrix would accuse him of in one of his jealous, yet heartbroken moments of anger. <br /><br />An important issue for me is to read the novel objectively, transcend back to that period, leave our current social warrior milieu behind, and experience the ambiance of the era. It was after all not meant as a futuristic view on our current society and should not be interpreted as such. <br /><br />There are some autobiographical elements present.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />NOTE FROM <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jan/26/100-best-novels-the-end-of-the-affair-graham-greene-observer-robert-mccrum" rel="nofollow noopener"><i><b>The Guardian</b></i></a>:<br /><br />The best clue to the emotional freight carried by The End of the Affair is probably to be found in its differing dedication pages. The English edition, published by William Heinemann in September 1951, reads “To C”. But the American edition, much less cryptic, reads “To Catherine with love”. Catherine Walston, the wife of the Labour peer Harry Walston, had been quite explicitly Greene’s mistress for several years, in a relationship that tormented all concerned. Few women ever touched Greene as deeply, however, and his novel became the sad record of their ultimately doomed relationship. “It was,” writes Norman Sherry in his very unsatisfactory three-volume biography, “a love affair of dangerous proportions”, and one wracked, as the novel is, with Catholic guilt.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The two characters who open the book, are also the very same people who walk out of the story in the end. In my humble opinion, this confirm the true intent of the book. If it was suppose to be otherwise, different characters would have opened and closed this saga.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>The novel began</u>
</b> where Maurice Bendrix met up with Henry Miles on a black wet January night on the Clapham Town Commons, in 1946. They haven't seen each other since June, 1944. Rivers of rain flowed through the city. It was hardly a night for anyone to be out and about in London. The war was ending, but from time to time air raids were still a stark reality. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>The story ended</u>
</b> with Maurice and Henry walking over the Common again to the Pontefract Arms pub. This time the skies were open, the lights were still out, but lovers met where the roads intersected. The end of the war was coming.<br /><br /><i> I called down to Henry, 'I'm ready,' and we walked side by side...<br /><br />'I look forward to these evenings of ours,' Henry said...<br /><br />'Yes.' ...<br /><br />'They are the only things I do look forward to,' Henry said.</i><br /><br />I thought it was an unusual story, beautifully narrated by Colin Firth in such a graceful, melodic flow.<br /><br />Maurice Bendrix started a new novel in 1938 and chose a senior civil servant as main character. He chose Henry as his model and befriended his wife Sarah. Henry was an important assistant secretary in the Ministry of Pensions - later it was to be the Ministry of Home Security. <br /><br />The story turned out to be about hate, more than love. But with that said, it didn't quite turn out that simple. The men had to conquer their own shortcomings, mistakes, emotional hell, and find a way back to the rest of their lives. <br /><br />Although the love affair was a main axle in the plot, it was by no means meant to be a love story. The main aim was the emotional turmoil of men in relationship with one women. The protagonist struggled with his own role in the events, and even battled Sarah's God, and lost. Actually, he wanted to battle everyone in Sarah's hemisphere. His jealousy and possessiveness knew no bounds. <br /><br />He said: <i>“I measured love by the extent of my jealousy.”</i><br /><br />As a result, the friendship and camaraderie between Bendrix and Henry became so much more remarkable. It is the reason why this book is highly recommended. The Guardian listed this novel as no. 71 of the best 100 novels of all times. I totally agree.<br /><br />A story written, brilliantly I might add, in the psychological realism style, the stream of consciousness-realm, addressing moral issues of the time. There were no winners or losers. It is one of those novels which I wanted to dwell in. For me it was a heartfelt, deeply emotional experience. A very good one indeed. (Okay, okay, I will confess. I have 'a thing' for Colin Firth too, now bite me :-)<br /><br />Nevertheless, I preferred to follow the real issue in the saga: the emotional journeys of the men who had to survive beautiful Sarah Miles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1846618084720438590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1683813302" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1683813302" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1683813302?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/13641539-the-end-of-the-affair">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Robin by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=english">english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1683813302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4290879012252112514"><i>I’m not at peace anymore. I just want him like I used to in the old days. I want to be eating sandwiches with him. I want to be drinking with him in a bar. I’m tired and I don’t want anymore pain. I want Maurice. <b>I want ordinary corrupt human love.</b> Dear God, you know I want to want Your pain, but I don’t want it now. Take it away for a while and give it me another time.</i> <br/><br/>I'm reeling in astonishment at this incredible work. Not only was Colin Firth's audio performance of this astounding (do yourse</span>
  <span id="freeText4290879012252112514" style="display:none"><i>I’m not at peace anymore. I just want him like I used to in the old days. I want to be eating sandwiches with him. I want to be drinking with him in a bar. I’m tired and I don’t want anymore pain. I want Maurice. <b>I want ordinary corrupt human love.</b> Dear God, you know I want to want Your pain, but I don’t want it now. Take it away for a while and give it me another time.</i> <br /><br />I'm reeling in astonishment at this incredible work. Not only was Colin Firth's audio performance of this astounding (do yourself a favour and listen - you will be transported), but the WRITING, oh the writing of this perfect novel had me in the first few paragraphs, knowing that this book would be one of my new favourites. I found myself going back to the print version to drool over phrases and chapters that were lingering with me.<br /><br />This is the story of Maurice Bendrix, a man consumed with hatred. His lover Sarah had called off their affair two years earlier during WW2 (her reasons unknown to him) and he is steeped in bitterness. By chance, he runs into Henry, Sarah's husband, and it re-ignites his hate and jealousy, so much so he hires a private eye to spy on her.<br /><br />We learn all about the affair, how it began, and Sarah's reasons for ending it. The love story alone is so compelling. The descriptions of their love and desire for each other are gorgeous and painful. Bendrix is a jealous lover, who loses trust the moment she is out of his sight. Their love is double-edged and so real. So ordinary, corrupt and human. So beautiful.<br /><br /><i>Sometimes I get tired of trying to convince him that I love him and shall love him for ever. He pounces on my words like a barrister and twists them. I know he is afraid of that desert which would be around him if our love were to end, but he can’t realize that I feel exactly the same. What he says aloud, I say to myself silently and write it here.</i><br /><br />But to the tale of passion Greene adds in a spiritual dimension that blows the story sky-high, giving readers so much to mull over. What is faith, what is divine intervention? Does God exist and hear our prayers? To what extent will He go to confirm and inspire belief? Or is it all in our heads? There are many crises in this book - those of the heart and those of the spirit. By bringing God into the equation, Greene created a four-way love story that gripped this reader's heart AND soul.<br /><br /><i>You needn't be so scared. Love doesn't end. Just because we don't see each other...</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4290879012252112514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2198693098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2198693098" />
    <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate"><img alt="Roger Brunyate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462877641p2/42294189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2198693098?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Brunyate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Brunyate" href="/user/show/42294189-roger-brunyate">Roger Brunyate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142437988" href="/book/show/394731.The_End_of_the_Affair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=illustrated-review">illustrated-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42294189-roger-brunyate?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2198693098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6966568790486943450"><b>Of Love and Hate</b><br/><br/>I first read this in 2007, and wrote a brief review on Amazon, which I reprint below. Reading it again now, ten years later, I very much stand by my original views and rating, but find myself fascinated by different things.<br/><br/>======<br/><br/>[<u>2007 review</u>] <br/><br/>I wish it were possible to read certain books for the first time without knowing how they will turn out. But publishers seem to imagine that once a book becomes a classic (and this is one of Greene's masterpieces) the plot becomes common pro</span>
  <span id="freeText6966568790486943450" style="display:none"><b>Of Love and Hate</b><br><br>I first read this in 2007, and wrote a brief review on Amazon, which I reprint below. Reading it again now, ten years later, I very much stand by my original views and rating, but find myself fascinated by different things.<br><br>======<br><br>[<u>2007 review</u>] <br><br>I wish it were possible to read certain books for the first time without knowing how they will turn out. But publishers seem to imagine that once a book becomes a classic (and this is one of Greene's masterpieces) the plot becomes common property. I read this in the Penguin Centennial edition, lovely to the eye and soft to the touch, yet the inside-cover blurb manages to reveal in two short paragraphs all the plot twists that Greene, as a master thriller writer, does not disclose until well into the second half of the book. You can see some reviewers on this site trying gamely not to let the cat out of the bag, but others (including the editorial reviews at the top) don't even try. So, if by some miracle you don't know how the novel turns out, stop reading NOW, buy the book, turn straight to page 1 without looking at the cover material, and enjoy. But fortunately, even if you do know the general outline, it is a finely written work of art which will give great pleasure anyhow.<br><br>The title is unusually apt. How daring to start a novel, literally, with the END of an affair! From this point where a novel might normally finish, Greene's character Maurice Bendrix begins not only to recall the course of his affair with Sarah Miles, the wife of a dull neighbor in the civil service, but also to gnaw on that word "end." Fueled by what he calls hate, but which we come to recognize as the obverse of his passion, he obsesses about how it ended, why it ended, and what happens after the end. Convinced that Sarah has left him for another man, he hires a private detective (Parkis, a surprisingly touching character) to follow her. Those who know Greene well may not find it hard to guess what Bendrix ultimately discovers, or that a rather sordid book about adultery would be transformed into a grueling challenge to the existence of God. It may in fact be the most explicitly religious of Greene's so-called Catholic novels, and too much for some readers, especially at the end. But it is a beautifully crafted work which never loses touch with the realities of this world (the Blitz, social conventions, physical sex) in its ultimate convergence on the next.<br><br>======<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1512302820i/24590349.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[<u>2017 comments</u>] <br><br>This time, I was immediately struck by the brilliant opening. On the first page alone, it establishes several things: <br><blockquote>1. That the protagonist, Maurice Bendrix, is a novelist, perhaps as close to Greene himself as he ever allowed.<br><br>2. That he will choose a shape for his story almost arbitrarily, and tell it in an unconventional way.<br><br>3. That, like <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48800.The_Third_Man" title="The Third Man by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Third Man</a>,</i> its immediate predecessor, it will take place in a <i>noir</i> world, heralded by "the black wet January night on the Common" with its "wide river of rain".<br><br>4. That this story about an affair is "a record of hate far more than of love."<br><br>5. And, in the apparently throwaway line "if I had believed then in a God," this book about adultery might turn out to occupy a different level entirely.</blockquote>It also struck me about how much Greene makes his points by their negatives. The obvious example is the love/hate dichotomy. I had assumed going in that any hatred would be just love under a different name, but I was quite surprised to find how much of it now seemed motivated, not by passion, but by meanness—which is especially painful since you want to like and respect Bendrix as a near self-portrait of the author himself. But having read most of his other major works, I know that the theme of hatred as the prelude to love is an essential part of Greene's theology, when it comes to a love for God. The model, of course, is the conversion on the road to Damascus of anti-Christian zealot Saul into the Apostle Paul, after years of "kicking against the pricks."<br><br>But here again, Greene is deceptive. The "if had known then what I know now" convention has become a novelist's cliché, and Bendrix's line in the opening paragraph, "if I had believed <i>then</i> in a God" [italics mine], might seem to imply that the novel will be about the conversion of Maurice Bendrix. And yet that turns out to be a major thread of suspense, one that Greene deliberately leaves unresolved for the reader to write his own ending. To me now, it is even more interesting than the obvious question of why Sarah Miles broke off their affair.<br><br>Reading again, I am more amazed than ever at Greene's technical audacity. We already know of his fondness for using a <i>noir</i> vehicle to deal with serious subjects. More interesting still is the way he uses the convention of the private detective to reveal inner truths as much as sordid facts. Then there are his tricks with time. He juggles three periods with consummate ease: just before the war when Maurice and Sarah first met, the war years in which they conducted their affair, and the postwar period well after the affair is over when Bendrix chooses to start his story. To start with the end, as I said before, is amazing in itself. But there is another end, even more surprising, that takes place two thirds of the way through; conventional novelists would have stopped there, but this is where Greene's true purpose, as a Catholic novelist (much like Evelyn Waugh), really begins. The way the five Books into which the novel is divided jump around by time and viewpoint is remarkable. The only thing I would criticize is the blatant device of having the detective enter the house and steal Sarah's journal as a way of having her tell part of the story in her own words—though the actual content of those words is superb.<br><br>The Penguin Centennial edition also has the benefit of a superb 16-page introduction by Michael Gorra. So so many book introductions, it gives a lot away, and so is best read as an afterword. But in that context, wow! Having twice read and twice reviewed this novel now, I thought I knew most of its angles. But Gorra is so thoughtful, so informed, so original in his own thinking—and so open to the fact that many readers will not like the religious content in Greene's novel—that reading him opens new vistas, and almost makes me want to start the book again, for the <i>third</i> time!<br><br>======<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1512302820i/24590350._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Finally, a shout-out for two other books. I found I had confused this in my memory with Elizabeth Bowen's <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1701739035" rel="nofollow noopener">The Heat of the Day</a>.</i> Her novel, also about an adulterous affair during the London Blitz, and also with <i>noir</i>-ish overtones, is described on its cover thus: "Imagine a Graham Greene thriller projected through the sensibility of Virginia Woolf." The Greene connection is what made me confuse the two in my memory, but on reading it again in 2016, I realized that the Virginia Woolf comparison is much more apt. I recommend it to anyone intrigued by this apparently impossible combination.<br><br>The second book is one of the few coffee-table volumes I have never regretted buying. Called <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/145042.Graham_Greene_Country" title="Graham Greene Country by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Graham Greene Country</a>,</i> it has been my indispensable companion to Greene ever since I picked one up on remainder; it is well worth getting if you can find a copy second-hand. In it, the English illustrator Paul Hogarth visits the sites of every one of the author's novels (which surely involved a considerable amount of globe-trotting!), describing his explorations and filling the pages with sketches in brilliant color; the illustrations to this review are from Hogarth's book. The commentary is by Greene himself. As one of Greene's greatest strengths is his sense of place, the combination of artist and author is a knockout. Only the synopses of each novel, accurate though they are, are likely to be spoilers for those who have not read that particular book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6966568790486943450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2183850704" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2183850704" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2183850704?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2183850704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10585687511730881506">Published in 1951, this is set in London during, and shortly after, the years of the Second World War and draws autobiographically on his own love affair with Lady Catherine Walston, which began in 1947. This book is more a record of hate, than of love, our central character, Maurice Bendrix tells us. Indeed, Bendrix is full of hate and anger, jealousy and spite. The novel begins in 1946, when Bendrix, an author, runs into Henry Miles on Clapham Common. Bendrix was having an affair with Henry’s </span>
  <span id="freeText10585687511730881506" style="display:none">Published in 1951, this is set in London during, and shortly after, the years of the Second World War and draws autobiographically on his own love affair with Lady Catherine Walston, which began in 1947. This book is more a record of hate, than of love, our central character, Maurice Bendrix tells us. Indeed, Bendrix is full of hate and anger, jealousy and spite. The novel begins in 1946, when Bendrix, an author, runs into Henry Miles on Clapham Common. Bendrix was having an affair with Henry’s wife, Sarah; but she ended the affair, leaving Bendrix an embittered and obsessive lover.<br /><br />This is a novel which is so realistic, so full of pain and rage, that you almost want to turn away from the page. Indeed, Bendrix is very much an anti-hero, full of unlikeable traits. He loves Sarah and longs for her to call him, but, when she is with him, he immediately wants to start a row with her. He is selfish, unkind, hurtful and yet, what saves him as a character, is that he knows he is behaving badly. He is all too aware of how unfair he is and how desperately he loves Sarah. Henry Miles is an important, successful Civil Servant, who cares for Sarah, but the couple have long lost the romantic side of their marriage. However, Bendrix is unaware of the real reasons why Sarah left him and his attempt to discover the truth will lead to disaster. <br /><br />Although this sounds a very depressing and dark novel, it has some tragic-comic moments; most notably in the character of the private detective, Parvis, and ‘his boy,’ Lance. The novel also delves into some huge, and important themes; the destructiveness of jealousy, the strange, reciprocal relationship, that grows between Henry and Bendrix, religion, vows and success. Through it all, Graham Greene lays bare his characters, and, through them, himself, with painful clarity. You cannot read this and not be moved. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10585687511730881506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1339990747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1339990747" />

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1339990747?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kohey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kohey" href="/user/show/8936504-kohey">kohey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1339990747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14255984484578797437">It's a true gem.I'm a Buddhist,so it's somewhat hard for me to understand some parts about Christianity. I've never read a book that tells me how deeply love can affect each other and how difficult to love or to be loved is. <br/>In this book,‘LOVE’and‘HATE’are never,ever mundane and cheap words,to be sure.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_739127139" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/739127139" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/739127139?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=libri-classici">libri-classici</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer739127139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4529417087216199864"><b>Just Let You Leave Without a Trace?</b><br/><br/>"How can you just walk away from me,<br/>When all I can do is watch you leave?<br/>Cause we've shared the laughter & the pain and even shared the tears<br/>You're the only one who really knew me at all."<br/>Phil Collins, <i>Against All Odds (Take a Look at Me Now)</i>, 1983.<br/><br/>As I'm trying to write a review worthy of this book, for some reason this damn song keeps replaying in my head. I guess it does sort of remind me of the plot of this book. [In any case, I MUST finish writing this as </span>
  <span id="freeText4529417087216199864" style="display:none"><b>Just Let You Leave Without a Trace?</b><br /><br />"How can you just walk away from me,<br />When all I can do is watch you leave?<br />Cause we've shared the laughter &amp; the pain and even shared the tears<br />You're the only one who really knew me at all."<br />Phil Collins, <i>Against All Odds (Take a Look at Me Now)</i>, 1983.<br /><br />As I'm trying to write a review worthy of this book, for some reason this damn song keeps replaying in my head. I guess it does sort of remind me of the plot of this book. [In any case, I MUST finish writing this as quickly as possible!]<br /><br /><br />Never has so short a book stirred in me such a cauldron of conflicting emotions.<br /><br />I think Sarah deserves love and passion, but my morals and religion cannot countenance it against her marital vow of commitment. I ponder, why not, in her marriage free from sex for seven years? Yet I cringe when I think certainly anyone is better than this Maurice Bendix, the Greene character which I imagine is him to a T: a pompous, neurotic, narcissistic jackass.<br /><br />Set during WW II, a bomb nearly misses killing Bendix at his flat. Sarah immediately breaks off the affair without an explanation. The rest of the story is a spoiler. <br /><br />This brilliant, short novel (the 4th of Greene's so-called Catholic novels) delves into questions of morals, faith in, and relationship with and to, God.<br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1389847372" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1389847372" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1389847372?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140184952" href="/book/show/1287447.The_End_of_the_Affair">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1389847372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13351041837127526058">This book was not at all what I expected. It took a turn that I could not have imagined and then it became about so much more than just an ending of a love affair, it became about love itself, about hate, about jealousy and indifference. And it became about religion and about God, which is an entirely different thing than religion itself. I felt pain and confusion and sorrow and anger. I was Bendrix and then I was Sarah, and I understood and misunderstood them equally. <br/><br/>Greene is a masterful stor</span>
  <span id="freeText13351041837127526058" style="display:none">This book was not at all what I expected. It took a turn that I could not have imagined and then it became about so much more than just an ending of a love affair, it became about love itself, about hate, about jealousy and indifference. And it became about religion and about God, which is an entirely different thing than religion itself. I felt pain and confusion and sorrow and anger. I was Bendrix and then I was Sarah, and I understood and misunderstood them equally. <br /><br />Greene is a masterful storyteller with a complete command of his craft. He teases us with making Bendrix a writer and describing how he writes and fashions a story, but there is something beneath the description that mingles truth and mendacity, just as they are mingled in the persons of Sarah and Bendrix. As I watched Bendrix swing between love and hate, I thought of Janus the God with two faces and realized that love and hate are the same emotion turned on its head. Have you ever truly hated someone that you did not love? It is too strong an emotion to waste on someone to whom you have been otherwise indifferent. It springs from love in the face of betrayal or severe disappointment, and it is love morphed into another guise and, as such, it can morph back again.<br /><br />I was initially confused about the relationship between Bendrix and Henry, but I do think that when we love and lose someone we feel tied to those who have also loved and lost. In the end, we control so little of what is truly deep emotion in us. Trying to control it is as futile as battling with God. God always wins.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13351041837127526058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cdaI2kx1bE22Y+/G+JvByLueZ6cp6+MQzLhPbBvIO3lL8OEI9yulJRFIW6cPbssXbcGAb+dmbXeXxgnd1ovJ4w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yzolT4RlHLAMRcswPlxmwVrH6W6Rrd/HwQcwdQyrmATxHEydPzvV2Ktuf1HJqWwejJgOpl8gUaCaeXbEwehqng==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Home is an entirely independent, deeply affecting novel that takes place concurrently in the same locale, this time in the household of Reverend Robert Boughton, Ames’s closest friend. \n\nGlory Boughton, aged thirty-eight, has returned to Gilead to care for her dying father. Soon her brother, Jack—the prodigal son of the family, gone for twenty years—comes home too, looking for refuge and trying to make peace with a past littered with tormenting trouble and pain. \n\nJack is one of the great characters in recent literature. A bad boy from childhood, an alcoholic who cannot hold a job, he is perpetually at odds with his surroundings and with his traditionalist father, though he remains Boughton’s most beloved child. Brilliant, lovable, and wayward, Jack forges an intense bond with Glory and engages painfully with Ames, his godfather and namesake. \n\nHome is a moving and healing book about families, family secrets, and the passing of the generations, about love and death and faith.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene">About Graham Greene</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1254688603p3/2533.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene">Graham Greene
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,451
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13088261986945027779">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br/><br/>Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a “Catholic novelist” rather than as a “novelist who happened to be Ca</span>
  <span id="freeText13088261986945027779" style="display:none">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br /><br />Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a “Catholic novelist” rather than as a “novelist who happened to be Catholic,” Catholic religious themes are at the root of much of his writing, especially the four major Catholic novels: <i>Brighton Rock, The Heart of the Matter, The End of the Affair,</i> and <i>The Power and the Glory.</i> Works such as <i>The Quiet American, Our Man in Havana</i> and <i>The Human Factor</i> also show an avid interest in the workings of international politics and espionage.<br /><br />(<i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graham_Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>)</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13088261986945027779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/267229-the-end-of-the-affair">Quotes from The End of the Af...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The sense of unhappiness is so much easier to convey than that of happiness. In misery we seem aware of our own existence, even though it may be in the form of a monstrous egotism: this pain of mine is individual, this nerve that winces belongs to me and to no other. But happiness annihilates us: we lose our identity.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
